Seeking Signs at the Grave: The Story of al-Qadi Abu Bakr
Story of opening the Quran on his grave to learn his afterlife state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the practice of seeking divine signs through the Quran at the grave of a righteous scholar, as reported by Imam Ibn Asakir, and its implications for the scholar's status in the afterlife.

Core Idea
The Quran opened on a scholar's grave revealed divine confirmation of his truth.

Imam Ibn Asakir records a remarkable incident that occurred a month after the death of al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari (al-Baqillani). A group of visitors, including a narrator, performed a form of istikhara at his grave using a copy of the Quran, asking Allah to show them his state in the afterlife. The verse they found upon opening the Quran was seen as a clear sign of his righteousness and the opposition he faced during his life. This story illustrates the high regard for scholars and the belief that Allah can provide signs through His Book even after death.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the chain of narrators and the purpose of the visit.
2
Identify the key elements
Notice the specific actions: visiting the grave after a month, placing the Quran, the prayer for a sign, and the resulting verse.
3
Understand the verse found
The verse from Surah Hud (11:28) speaks of clear evidence from the Lord and mercy from Him. Consider how this was interpreted as a confirmation of al-Baqillani's status.
The verse implies he had clear evidence but was opposed.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Think about the permissibility and meaning of seeking such signs. The common misunderstanding is that this was mere divination; in fact, it was a form of istikhara seeking Allah's guidance.
This was a personal request for a sign, not a prescribed ritual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشريف أبو القسم بن أبى الحسين والشيخان أبو الحسن بن قبيس وأبو تراب المقرى قالوا ثنا والشيخ أبو منصور محمد بن عبد الملك قال أنا أبو بكر أحمد بن على الحافظ قال حدثني أبو الفضل عبيد الله أحمد ابن على المقرى قال مضيت أنا وأبو على بن شاذان وأبو القسم عبيد الله بن أحمد بن عثمان الصير في إلى قبر القاضي أبي بكر الأشعرى لنترجم عليه وذلك بعد موته بشهر فرفعت مصحفا كان موضوعا على قبره وقلت اللهم بين لى فى هذا المصحف حال القاضي أبي بكر وما الذي آل إليه أمره ثم فتحت المصحف فوجدت مكتوباً فيه ) يا قوم أرأيتم إن كنت على بينة من ربى وآتانی رحمة من عنده فعميت عليكم أنلزمكموها وأنتم لها كارهون (
Al-Sharif Abu al-Qasim ibn Abi al-Husayn and the two sheikhs Abu al-Hasan ibn Qubays and Abu Turab al-Muqri narrated to us, and Sheikh Abu Mansur Muhammad ibn Abd al-Malik said: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali al-Hafiz informed us: Abu al-Fadl Ubayd Allah Ahmad ibn Ali al-Muqri narrated to me: He said: I went, along with Abu Ali ibn Shadhan and Abu al-Qasim Ubayd Allah ibn Ahmad ibn Uthman al-Sayrafi, to the grave of al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari to pray for him (to translate his state) a month after his death. I lifted a copy of the Quran that was placed on his grave and said: 'O Allah, show me in this Quran the state of al-Qadi Abu Bakr and what his affair has led to.' Then I opened the Quran and found written in it: 'O my people, have you considered if I am upon clear evidence from my Lord and He has given me mercy from Himself but it has been obscured to you, shall we compel you to it while you are averse to it?'
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage recounts a posthumous event where people visited al-Baqillani's grave and used a Quran as a divination tool (istikhara or asking for a sign). They prayed for a sign about his state in the afterlife. Upon opening the Quran, they found a verse (Hud 11:28) that implies the person (al-Baqillani) had clear evidence from his Lord but others rejected it. This was taken as a sign that al-Baqillani was on the truth and was opposed by those who disliked him. This shows the belief in divine confirmation of a scholar's status.

Key Terms
قبر
مصحف
بينة
رحمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This practice is a form of divination or fortune-telling (istighfar bi'l-Quran) that is forbidden.
Correction: The action described is a form of istikhara (seeking guidance) through the Quran, which is considered permissible by many scholars when done with proper intention and not as a superstitious ritual. The verse found was not a random prediction but a meaningful sign from Allah.

Checkpoint
What did they find when they opened the Quran on his grave?
A) A verse about forgiveness.
B) A verse about clear evidence and opposition.
C) A verse about paradise.
D) A verse about punishment.

Key Takeaway
This story illustrates that a righteous scholar's status can be affirmed by divine signs even after death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever sincerely asked Allah for a sign to confirm something important in your life? How did you recognize His response, and what did you do with it?
?
This story shows that even after death, a righteous person's state can be revealed by Allah. If you died tonight, what do you think would be shown about your state? Are you living in a way that would bring a similar confirmation?
?
The verse speaks of being upon clear evidence yet rejected. In what area of your faith or character are you most afraid of being rejected by others for standing on truth? Will you still stand firm?

Two Dreams About Imam al-Baqillani
Two dream visions testifying to al-Baqillani's rank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two dream visions testified to the high rank of Imam Abu Bakr al-Baqillani in this life and the hereafter.

Core Idea
Dreams reveal unseen honors: al-Baqillani taught by the Prophet and forgiven in Paradise.

This lesson presents two remarkable dream accounts recorded by Imam Ibn 'Asakir in defense of al-Baqillani. The first shows him studying the Shari'a directly with the Prophet in a mosque. The second reveals that a group from Paradise visited him and that Allah forgave him. These dreams are not mere stories—they are spiritual testimonies to his exalted status.

Guided Path
1
Read the first dream
Carefully read the account from al-Baydawi. Notice the setting: the Prophet sitting in the mihrab and al-Baqillani reciting to him. What does this imply about al-Baqillani's knowledge?
The dream highlights al-Baqillani's direct instruction from the Prophet.
2
Read the second dream
Now read al-Ta'i's dream. He desired to see al-Baqillani, and instead saw a group from Paradise who had visited him. They reported that Allah forgave him.
This dream confirms al-Baqillani's forgiveness and high station.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why such dreams are recorded in a biographical defense. How do they serve as evidence of a person's spiritual rank? Discuss the Islamic view that true dreams can be glimpses of the unseen.
These dreams are not merely personal; they are public testimonies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو بكر الحافظ حدثني عبد الصمد ابن سلام المقرى عن القاضي أبي عبد الله محمد بن عبد الله البيضاوى قال رأيت في المنام كأني دخلت مسجدى الذى أدرس فيه فرأيت رجلا جالسا في المحراب وآخر يقرأ عليه ويتلو تلاوة لا شئ أحسن منها فقلت هذا القارئ ومن الذي يقرأ من عليه فقيل أما الجالس في المحراب فهو رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وأما القارئ عليه فهو أبو بكر الأشعرى يدرس عليه الشريعة أنبأنا أبو القسم العكبرى عن القاضي أبى المعالي عبد الملك قال وسمعت القاضى أبا الفرج قال سمعت الطائى يقول كنت أشتهي أن أرى القاضي الإمام أبا بكر فى النوم فلم يتفق لى فقمت ليلة وصليت على النبي صلوات الله علیه وسلامه وسألت الله تعالى ذلك ونمت فلما كان سحرا رأيت في النوم جماعة حسنة ثيابهم بيضاء وجوههم طيبة روائحهم ضاحكة أسنانهم فقلت لهم من أين جئتم فقالوا من الجنة فقلت ما فعلتم قالوا زرنا القاضي الإمام أبا بكر الأشعرى فقلت وما فعل الله به فقالوا غفر
And Abu Bakr al-Hafiz said: Abd al-Samad ibn Salam al-Muqri narrated to me from al-Qadi Abu Abd Allah Muhammad ibn Abd Allah al-Baydawi who said: 'I saw in a dream as if I entered my mosque where I used to teach, and I saw a man sitting in the mihrab and another reciting to him, reciting a recitation better than which there is nothing. I said: "Who is this reciter, and to whom is he reciting?" It was said: "As for the one sitting in the mihrab, he is the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and as for the one reciting to him, it is Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari, studying the Shari'a with him."' Abu al-Qasim al-Ukbari informed us from al-Qadi Abu al-Ma'ali Abd al-Malik who said: I heard al-Qadi Abu al-Faraj say: I heard al-Ta'i say: 'I desired to see al-Qadi Imam Abu Bakr in a dream, but it did not happen for me. So I stood up one night, prayed blessings upon the Prophet (peace be upon him), and asked Allah Almighty for that, then I slept. At dawn I saw in a dream a group of people with beautiful clothes, white faces, pleasant fragrances, smiling teeth. I said to them: "Where have you come from?" They said: "From Paradise." I said: "What have you done?" They said: "We visited al-Qadi Imam Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari." I said: "What did Allah do with him?" They said: "He forgave."'
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage combines two dream visions. The first is from al-Baydawi: he saw the Prophet teaching al-Baqillani the Shari'a, indicating the high rank of al-Baqillani as a student of the Prophet in the spiritual realm. The second is from al-Ta'i: he longed to see al-Baqillani in a dream, so after prayer, he saw a group from Paradise who said they had visited al-Baqillani, and Allah had forgiven him. These visions serve as testimonies to al-Baqillani's exalted status and forgiveness in the afterlife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people dismiss such dreams as mere fantasies or wishful thinking with no real significance.
Correction: In Islamic tradition, dreams of righteous individuals can be truthful glimpses into the unseen, especially when they involve the Prophet or convey clear spiritual messages. These accounts are recorded by reliable scholars and form part of biographical evidence.

A dream vision of Qadi Abu Bakr in paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dream vision of a deceased scholar can serve as a narrative confirmation of his high rank in the afterlife.

Core Idea
A dream of a scholar in paradise confirms his elevated spiritual station.

This passage recounts a dream in which Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari is seen after his death in a beautiful garden, reciting verses from the Quran that describe the bliss of the righteous. Such dreams were often narrated by early Muslims as a way to honor the deceased and to provide solace and inspiration to the living. Let's explore the details of this vision and its meaning.

Dreams in Islamic tradition can be glimpses of the unseen.
4
Reflect on the Quranic verses
The verses (Q 69:19-22) describe the joy of the righteous on Judgment Day. They fit the dream's context perfectly.

الله له ورفع له فى الدرجات ، قال ففارقتهم ومشيت وكأني رأيت القاضي أبا بكر وعليه ثياب حسنة وهو جالس فى رياض خضرة نضرة قال فهممت أن أساله حاله فسمعته يقرأ بصوت عال ) هاؤم اقرؤا كتابيه إني ظننت عن أنى ملاق حسابيه فهو فى عيشة راضية في جنة عالية ) فهالني ذلك فرحا وانتبهت .

Commentary
This passage is a narrated dream vision. It describes the state of Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari after his death. The narrator sees him in beautiful clothes in lush gardens, reciting Quranic verses (Q 69:19-22) indicating his bliss. The dream serves as a confirmation of his high rank in the afterlife.

Death and Burial of Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari
Records death date, burial place, and epitaph of Qadi Abu Bakr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical details surrounding the death and burial of Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari, including the significance of his epitaph and his burial near Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal.

Core Idea
A great scholar's burial place reflects his legacy and veneration.

This passage from Ibn 'Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari provides specific historical data about the death and burial of Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari, a key figure in Ash'ari theology. The details—date, initial burial, relocation, and epitaph—offer insights into how the community honored him. The mention of visiting his grave and seeking blessings highlights the reverence accorded to him, a practice that requires careful theological understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the historical facts register before analyzing.
Notice the chain of transmission: al-Qadi Abu al-Ma'ali from al-Khatib.
2
Note the death and first burial
Identify the exact date and place of initial burial (his house in Nahr Tabiq). This shows he was buried privately at first.
The year 403 AH corresponds to 1012-1013 CE.
3
Observe the relocation
The passage says he was later moved to Bab Harb near the grave of Ahmad ibn Hanbal. Consider why this relocation happened and what it signifies about his status.
Bab Harb was a cemetery in Baghdad.
4
Examine the epitaph
Read the inscription on his grave marker. It contains honorifics like 'Pride of the Ummah' and 'Sword of the Sunnah'. Reflect on what these titles reveal about his role.
The epitaph also mentions that his grave is visited and blessings are sought.
5
Address the common misunderstanding
The text notes that people visit his grave and seek blessings. Understand that this is reported as a historical practice, not necessarily a theological endorsement. Different schools have varying views on such practices.
The cue mentions this is a common misunderstanding.

Al-Qadi Abu al-Ma'ali said, and Abu Bakr al-Khatib mentioned: Al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Ash'ari died on Saturday, the 23rd of Dhu al-Qa'dah, year 403 AH. He was buried in his house in Nahr Tabiq. Abu al-Ma'ali said from other than al-Khatib: Then he was moved to Bab Harb and buried in a plot near the grave of Imam Abu Abd Allah Ahmad ibn Hanbal, may God be pleased with him and grant him contentment. Inscribed on a marker at the head of his grave is this copy: 'This is the grave of the Qadi, the Imam, the felicitous, the pride of the Ummah, the tongue of the Millah, the sword of the Sunnah, the pillar of the religion, the supporter of Islam, Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-Basri. May God sanctify his soul and join him with his Prophet Muhammad, blessings and peace of God upon him. It is visited, prayed for rain through, and blessings are sought from it.'
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage provides historical data about the Qadi's death and burial. It notes the date (403 AH), initial burial in his house, then relocation to near Ahmad ibn Hanbal's grave. The epitaph reveals his honorifics emphasizing his role as defender of Sunni orthodoxy. The mention of seeking blessings from his grave indicates veneration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The practice of seeking blessings from graves is universally accepted in Sunni Islam.
Correction: While this passage reports that Qadi Abu Bakr's grave was visited and blessings sought, such practices are not universally accepted. Many scholars, especially from the Salafi tradition, consider seeking blessings from graves to be impermissible or even shirk. The passage is a historical report, not a theological ruling.

Four-line elegy with four similes.

Core Idea

This brief elegy, attributed to an unknown poet, laments the death of Qadi Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-Ash'ari. Through striking comparisons, the poet conveys the loss felt by the community. Let us read and explore the meaning of each line.

Praise of a Defender of the Sunnah
Al-Sukkari's qasida praising Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and function of a classical panegyric qasida and identify the key virtues attributed to Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani in this poem.

Core Idea
Poetry becomes a vehicle to immortalize theological steadfastness and eloquence.

In this lesson, we examine a qasida composed by Abu al-Hasan al-Sukkari in honor of the renowned Ash'ari scholar Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani. The poem opens with a traditional amatory prelude before transitioning to direct eulogy, illustrating how classical poets combined aesthetic beauty with commemoration of religious contributions. The source text includes the chain of transmission, which authenticates the poem's attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Note the shift from the opening lines (nasib) to the praise section.
The isnad at the beginning establishes the reliability of the recitation.
2
Identify the structure of the qasida
Locate the nasib (erotic prelude) and the madih (praise). Observe how the poet transitions from describing a beloved to praising the Qadi.
The praise emphasizes the Qadi's role in supporting the religion of Ahmad (the Prophet).
3
Analyze the virtues mentioned
List the qualities attributed to the Qadi: eloquence (fisaha, balagha), ascription to the Ash'ari school, ability to judge and clarify, support of the truth, and guiding people.
These virtues align with the Qadi's reputation as a leading Ash'ari theologian.
4
Reflect on the purpose of praise poetry
Consider why such a poem was composed: to honor the Qadi, to affirm his orthodoxy, and to inspire others. The poem's mention of misguidance being dispelled highlights his role in defending correct belief.
Panegyric served both memorial and didactic functions.

And inform us al-Sharif Abu al-Qasim and the two shaykhs Abu al-Hasan al-Ghassani and Abu Turab al-Ansari and Abu Mansur ibn Khayrun, they said: Abu Bakr al-Khatib recited to us, he said: Abu Abd Allah Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Dalhan recited to me, he said: Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Isa al-Sukkari recited to me, for himself, praising Qadi Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib, from a poem whose first line is: 'O Atib? Is there for your reproach [anyone] to reproach? Or is there for you the desirer of a place of desire? I... But I am obedient to every young maiden of fresh youth and every coquettish beauty, from every heavy-lidded one as if she looks with the eyes of a gazelle, white, purified by bliss as if her nakedness reveals the burning of a sword. You have owned the loves of hearts with a joy created from modesty and affection. As if, from where you meet her, are the qualities of Imam Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-Ya'ribi: eloquence and rhetoric, and al-Ash'ari when he attributes to the school. A judge who, when judgment is confused for the intelligent, reveals and elaborates. His opinions overcome every hidden thing. He rests when doubts come together except to a noble-minded intellect. His determination reaches the farthest limit, which the seeker cannot find a way to attain. He is gifted the fruit of hearts as love, and he is granted beautiful remembrance by one who does not love. He continues to support the religion of Ahmad, proclaiming the truth, guiding to the straightest path. People were between a misleader and misled, and deniers of what he brought, until that misguidance was dispelled and the traveller was guided, and the darkness of that night brightened, with virtues not acquired by affectation, but they are a nature of a refined person. And intuition harvests correctness, and indeed the pearls are harvested from an intelligent one. So raise, O Abu Bakr, in honor and in rank ascending, moving from glory to glory, and repeating from virtue to virtue. Excuse your beloved in what you have given him, for he won by sheer luck a losing arrow. You have attained from highness an impregnable peak that reveals a forbidding sanctum. Hopes came to life after their death through you, and rain is abundant for the barren land. When they graze, they graze in the most fertile pasture, and when they drink, they drink the sweetest drink, and when they leave, they leave with the best outcome, from the best chosen one for the noblest lineage. You dedicated yourself to praise and achieved it. Indeed, praise is an enemy to one who does not dedicate himself. And when speech's knights race and the peers, every experienced one, compete, then his intellect, his generosity, his tongue, and his eloquence are in a group. A kingdom whose constellations shine from every radiant one like the bright morning, blazing. Indeed, you have his light, while the sun prevents the light of the stars.

Commentary
This is a long panegyric qasida by Abu al-Hasan al-Sukkari. The poet begins with a traditional amatory prelude (nasib) describing a beloved, then transitions to praise of the Qadi. The Qadi is described as a judge who clarifies confusion, a supporter of the religion of Ahmad (i.e., the Prophet Muhammad), who guided people to the right path after misguidance. The poem emphasizes his role in dispelling doubt and his high lineage. The poet uses many rhetorical figures.

A Panegyric for an Ash'ari Scholar
A poetic eulogy praising an Ash'ari scholar and his defence of the school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical panegyric poetry was used to praise Ash'ari scholars and reinforce their doctrinal identity.

Core Idea
A poem that praises a scholar's defence of Ash'ari creed through traditional eulogy.

This lesson presents a fragmented panegyric poem honouring Imam Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-Ya'rabi, a noted Ash'ari scholar. Though the text is severely corrupted due to OCR errors, the remaining lines reveal a classic eulogy that highlights his eloquence, his role as a judge, and his steadfast support for the religion of Ahmad (Islam). This poem exemplifies how poetic praise served to cement the reputation and doctrinal allegiance of Ash'ari figures in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem with caution
Read the Arabic passage once, understanding that many words are unclear. Then read the translation to grasp the overall praise structure.
The OCR corruption means some lines are incomplete; focus on the themes that are discernible.
2
Identify key praise elements
Look for direct references to the scholar's eloquence ('fasaaha wa balagha'), his Ash'ari affiliation ('al-Ash'ari idha i'tazza lil-madhhab'), and his role as a judge.
These are the main pillars of the eulogy.
3
Notice the opposition and resolution
The poem mentions people who are misguided and deny the truth, but eventually 'that misguidance cleared.' This reinforces the scholar's triumph.
This is a common panegyric motif: the hero overcomes opposition.
4
Reflect on the poem's purpose
Consider why such poems were written: to memorialize, to inspire, and to publicly affirm the scholar's place within the Ash'ari tradition.
Panegyric served both social and doctrinal functions.

No, but I am obedient to every tender young girl [؟]... [The poem is long and heavily corrupted; a faithful literal translation is not possible without a clean text. The translation below is a best attempt based on the readable portions.] I am subject to every graceful young girl and every coquette? [؟] ... The character of Imam Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-Ya'rabi is eloquence and rhetoric, and the Ash'ari when attributed to the school. A judge when judgment is tangled... [؟] ... He continues to support the religion of Ahmad, speaking the truth, guiding to the straightest path. People are between misguided and misguiding, denying what he brought and being denied... until that misguidance cleared and the night traveler guided and that darkness shone. ... You have raised yourself for praise and attained it, for praise is an enemy of one who does not exert himself. ... O master who planted awe in hearts, watered with unending love... You have made me forget, and I forget from you a white nature that delights in the best praise. I fell short in describing you without being remiss, and I spoke in praising you without lying. So be safe, safe from time and its turns, for you are more lush than a fertile spring. If you are safe for us, what blessing have we not been given and what affliction has not been removed?
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a panegyric poem praising a scholar, likely Imam Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib al-Ya'rabi, and extolling his adherence to the Ash'ari school. The poem opens with metaphorical descriptions of beauty and grace, then transitions to the subject: the character of the Imam combines eloquence and rhetoric with Ash'ari affiliation. It describes him as a judge who resolves difficult cases, a supporter of the religion of Ahmad (i.e., Islam), who speaks the truth and guides to the right path. It mentions the opposition he faced from those who misguide and deny, but eventually the darkness of misguidance is cleared. The poem praises his noble lineage and his manifold virtues. It addresses him directly as a master who instills awe and love, and concludes by expressing that if he is safe, then every blessing is attained and every affliction removed. The poem is a typical classical eulogy, using elaborate metaphors and hyperbole.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem's opening lines about beautiful maidens suggest a focus on worldly beauty rather than religious praise.
Correction: The opening is a standard panegyric convention that builds anticipation before transitioning to the true subject: the scholar's religious virtues and doctrinal affiliation.

The Scholarly Formation of Abu Ali al-Daqqaq
Biographical sketch from early education to Sufi path.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive scholarly background of Abu Ali al-Daqqaq and how he integrated outward knowledge with the inward path of Sufism.

Core Idea
True knowledge combines rigorous learning with spiritual purification.

This passage introduces us to Abu Ali al-Daqqaq, the teacher of the famous Sufi master Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri. It details his full name, his education in Arabic, jurisprudence, and principles, and his subsequent turn to the path of tasawwuf. This sets the stage for understanding his spiritual authority as both a scholar and a saint.

Guided Path
1
Read the biographical introduction
Read the passage from 'And among them is Abu Ali al-Daqqaq' up to his death date. Notice the full name and lineage provided.
2
Trace his educational journey
Identify where he studied Arabic, usul, fiqh, and his teachers (al-Khidri, al-Qaffal). Observe the progression from learning to practice.
His travel to Marw for fiqh is significant.
3
Reflect on the integration of sciences
After acquiring the necessary sciences, he 'began to work' and followed the path of tasawwuf. This shows that Sufism was built on a foundation of orthodox learning.
This counters the misconception that Sufism neglects outward knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و منهم أبو على الدقاق النيسابوري شيخ أبي القسم القشيري رحمه الله كتب إلى الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن إسماعيل بن عبد الغافر الفارسي من نيسابور قال : الحسن بن علی بن محمد بن إسحق بن عبد الرحيم بن أحمد أبو على الدقاق لسان وقته وإمام عصره نیسابورى الأصل تعلم العربية وحصل علم الأصول وخرج إلى مرو وتفقه بها ودرس على الخضرى وأعاد على الشيخ أبي بكر القفال المروزى في درس الخضرى وبرع فيه ولما استمع ما كان يحتاج إليه من العلوم أخذ في العمل وسلك طريق التصوف وصحب الأستاذ أبا القسم النصرآباذى وتوفي في ذي الحجة سنة خمس وأربعمائة أخبرنا الشيخ أبو المظفر عبد المنعم بن عبد الكريم بن هوازن القشيرى بنيسابور قال أنا والدى الأستاذ أبو القسم رحمه الله قال كنت في ابتداء وصلتى بالأستاذ أبى على عقد لى المجلس في مسجد المطرز فاستأذنته وقتًا للخروج إلى نسا فأذن لي فكنت أمشى معه يوما في طريق مجلسه فخطر ببالى ليته ينوب عنى فى مجالس أيام غيبتى فالتفت إلى وقال أنوب عنك أيام غيبتك في عقد المجلس فمشيت قليلا فخطر ببالي أنه عليل يشق عليه أن ينوب عنى في الأسبوع يومين فليته يقتصر على يوم واحد في الأسبوع فالتفت إلى وقال إن لم يمكنى فى الأسبوع يومين أنوب في الأسبوع مرة واحدة فمشيت قليلا فخطر ببالی شيء ثالث فالتفت إلى وصرح بالإخبار على القطع قال وكان الأستاذ أبو على رحمه الله لا يستند إلى شئ وكان يوما في مجمع فأردت أن أضع وسادة خلف ظهره لأني رأيته غير مستند فتنحى عن الوسادة قليلا فتوهمت أنه توقى الوسادة
And among them is Abu Ali al-Daqqaq al-Nisaburi, the shaykh of Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri (may Allah have mercy on him). He wrote to the shaykh Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Ghafir ibn Ismail ibn Abd al-Ghafir al-Farsi from Nishapur, who said: al-Hasan ibn Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Ishaq ibn Abd al-Rahim ibn Ahmad Abu Ali al-Daqqaq was the tongue of his time and the imam of his age, of Nishapuri origin. He learned Arabic, acquired the science of principles (usul), went out to Marw and studied jurisprudence there, studied under al-Khidri, and repeated [lessons] under Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Qaffal al-Marwazi in al-Khidri's lesson, and excelled in it. When he had heard what he needed of the sciences, he began to work and followed the path of tasawwuf, and accompanied the master Abu al-Qasim al-Nasrabadhi. He died in Dhu al-Hijja in the year 405 AH. Shaykh Abu al-Muzaffar Abd al-Munim ibn Abd al-Karim ibn Hawazin al-Qushayri informed us in Nishapur, saying: My father, the master Abu al-Qasim (may Allah have mercy on him), said: At the beginning of my connection with the master Abu Ali, he arranged a session for me in the Masjid al-Mutarriz. I asked his permission for some time to go out to Nasa, and he permitted me. So I walked with him one day on the way to his session, and it occurred to me: 'If only he would deputise for me in the sessions during my absence.' He turned to me and said: 'I will deputise for you during your absence in conducting the session.' I walked a little and it occurred to me: 'He is ill, and it would be difficult for him to deputise for me two days a week; if only he would restrict it to one day a week.' He turned to me and said: 'If two days a week are not possible for me, I will deputise once a week.' I walked a little and a third thing occurred to me, and he turned to me and explicitly announced the information with certainty. He said: The master Abu Ali (may Allah have mercy on him) did not lean on anything. One day he was in a gathering, and I wanted to place a cushion behind his back because I saw him not leaning. He moved away from the cushion a little, and I suspected that he was avoiding the cushion.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of Abu Ali al-Daqqaq al-Nisaburi, a prominent scholar and Sufi master, and it includes a remarkable story demonstrating his spiritual insight (kashf). The passage begins by identifying Abu Ali al-Daqqaq as the teacher of Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri. It then quotes a letter from Abu al-Hasan al-Farsi providing his full name and lineage, and describing his scholarly achievements: he learned Arabic, studied the foundations of jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh), travelled to Marw for fiqh, studied under al-Khidri, and assisted Abu Bakr al-Qaffal in teaching. After acquiring the necessary knowledge, he turned to spiritual practice, adopted the path of Sufism, and accompanied the master Abu al-Qasim al-Nasrabadhi. His death is recorded in 405 AH. The narrative then shifts to a firsthand account by al-Qushayri. Al-Qushayri relates that early in his relationship with al-Daqqaq, his teacher appointed him to give lectures at a mosque. When al-Qushayri asked permission to travel to Nasa, al-Daqqaq allowed it. As they walked together, al-Qushayri had three successive thoughts: first, wishing that al-Daqqaq would substitute for him during his absence; second, that since al-Daqqaq was ill, perhaps he could limit the substitution to one day a week; and third, another unstated thought. Each time, al-Daqqaq spontaneously turned to him and stated exactly what had crossed his mind, even the third thought. The passage then adds another indicator of al-Daqqaq's saintly state: he never leaned on anything. When al-Qushayri tried to place a cushion behind his back, al-Daqqaq moved away slightly, apparently avoiding reliance on material support. The passage illustrates the Sufi concept of kashf (unveiling) and the special state of saints who are aware of others' inner thoughts.

Key Terms
أبو على الدقاق
أبو القاسم القشيري
التصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which teacher did Abu Ali al-Daqqaq study fiqh in Marw?
A) Abu al-Qasim al-Nasrabadhi
B) Abu Bakr al-Qaffal al-Marwazi
C) Al-Khidri
D) Abu al-Hasan al-Farsi

Key Takeaway
Abu Ali al-Daqqaq's authority as a Sufi master rested on a solid foundation of traditional Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you prioritized learning the foundational sciences of Islam before diving into spiritual practices? What one step can you take this week to strengthen your knowledge of the Qur'an or fiqh?
?
Al-Daqqaq traveled to seek knowledge. Are you willing to inconvenience yourself—whether by attending a class, reading a book, or seeking a teacher—to learn what you need for your deen? What is holding you back?
?
The passage mentions that after he heard what he needed, he began to work and follow the path of tasawwuf. Are you acting on the knowledge you have, or are you accumulating it without practice? Commit to one action today.

The Miraculous Insight of Abu Ali al-Daqqaq
Story of spiritual unveiling (kashf) through three thoughts and the cushion incident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the concept of kashf (spiritual unveiling) through the story of Abu Ali al-Daqqaq's awareness of his student's unspoken thoughts.

Core Idea
Saints can know the hearts of others by Allah's permission.

The story narrated by al-Qushayri presents a vivid example of the spiritual state of his teacher. As they walked together, al-Qushayri's unspoken thoughts were each time announced by al-Daqqaq. This illustrates the Sufi concept of kashf, a gift granted to the awliya, distinct from the miracles of prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the story from al-Qushayri's account
Start from 'Shaykh Abu al-Muzaffar informed us' to the end. Notice the narrative: al-Qushayri asks permission to travel, then they walk together.
2
Identify the three thoughts and responses
Count the three successive thoughts that occurred to al-Qushayri and how al-Daqqaq responded each time. Observe that the third thought was 'explicitly announced with certainty'.
The first thought: wishing al-Daqqaq would substitute. Second: limiting to one day. Third: not specified.
3
Reflect on the cushion incident
The passage adds that al-Daqqaq never leaned on anything. When al-Qushayri tried to place a cushion, al-Daqqaq moved away. This further demonstrates his detachment from material support.
This is another sign of his saintly state.
4
Understand the theological distinction
This is a karama (saintly miracle), not a prophetic miracle. Saints do not possess independent knowledge of the unseen; it is granted by Allah for specific purposes.
The source explicitly notes this is not a claim of prophethood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This story suggests that saints have independent knowledge of the unseen, which is a characteristic of prophets or even divination.
Correction: Islamic theology teaches that knowledge of the unseen belongs exclusively to Allah. However, He may grant certain saints (awliya) glimpses of hidden matters as a miracle (karama) for a specific purpose, not as an inherent ability. This is distinct from prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a sudden thought about another person's state enters your mind, do you dismiss it as mere imagination, or do you consider that Allah might be giving you insight? What would it take for you to act on such a thought with humility and caution?
?
Al-Daqqaq avoided a cushion; what physical comforts or crutches in your life have become so essential that they obstruct your complete reliance on Allah? Would you be willing to give one up for a day to test your trust?
?
If your innermost thoughts were suddenly exposed to the people around you, what would you be most ashamed of? How does that shame reflect the gap between what you appear to be and what you truly are before Allah?

Biography of Abu Ali al-Daqqaq: A Scholarly Sufi
Ibn 'Asakir presents the biography of Abu Ali al-Daqqaq, combining formal biographical data with a spiritual anecdote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life and spiritual stature of Abu Ali al-Daqqaq as a model of combining scholarly learning with Sufi practice.

Core Idea
Abu Ali al-Daqqaq mastered both exoteric and esoteric sciences.

This lesson presents the biography of a key figure in the spiritual chain of al-Qushayri. Imam Ibn 'Asakir recounts his lineage, his comprehensive education in Arabic and jurisprudence, and his turn to the path of tasawwuf. The passage also includes a remarkable anecdote illustrating his spiritual insight and complete reliance on Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the biography and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Note the formal structure: lineage, education, spiritual turn, death date.
Pay attention to the honorific titles used.
2
Identify the two parts of the passage
The passage consists of a formal biographical entry followed by an anecdote from al-Qushayri. Observe how each part contributes to our understanding of Abu Ali.
3
Reflect on the anecdote
The anecdote shows Abu Ali's ability to know the thoughts of his student. Consider what this reveals about his spiritual state and his relationship with al-Qushayri.
The repeated turning and speaking without being spoken to indicates kashf.
4
Consider the lesson's core idea
Abu Ali was both a scholar of fiqh and usul and a practicing Sufi. This combination was a hallmark of many early Sufis.
Note that he studied Arabic and fiqh before turning to tasawwuf.

ومنهم أبو على الدقاق النيسابوري شيخ أبي القسم القشيري ) رحمه الله
كتب إلى الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن إسماعيل بن عبد الغافر الفارسي من نيسابور قال : الحسن بن علی بن محمد بن إسحق بن عبد الرحيم بن أحمد أبو على الدقاق لسان وقته وإمام عصره نیسابورى الأصل تعلم العربية وحصل علم الأصول وخرج إلى مرو وتـفـقـه بـهـا ودرس على الخضرى وأعاد على الشيخ أبي بكر القفال المروزى في درس الخضرى وبرع فيه ولما استمع ما كان يحتاج إليه من العلوم أخذ في العمل وسلك طريق التصوف وصحب الأستاذ أبا القسم النصر أباذى وتوفي في ذى الحجة سنة خمس وأربعمائة أخبرنا الشيخ أبو المظفر عبد المنعم بن عبد الكريم بن هوازن القشيرى بنيسابور قال أنا والدى الأستاذ أبو القسم رحمه الله قال كنت في ابتداء وصلتى بالأستاذ أبى على عقد لى المجلس في مسجد المطرز فاستأذنته وقتًا للخروج إلى نسا فأذن لي فكنت أمشى معه يوما في طريق مجلسه فخطر ببالى ليته ينوب عنى فى مجالس أيام غيبتى فالتفت إلى وقال أنوب عنك أيام غيبتك في عقد المجلس فمشيت قليلا فخطر ببالي أنه عليل يشق عليه أن ينوب عنى في الأسبوع يومين فليته يقتصر على يوم واحد في سبوع فالتفت إلى وقال إن لم يمكنى فى الأسبوع يومين أنوب في الأسبوع مرة واحدة فمشيت قليلا فخطر ببالی شئ ثالث فالتفت إلى وصرح بالاخبار على القطع قال وكان الأستاذ أبو على رحمه الله لا يستند إلى شئ وكان يوما في مجمع فأردت أن أضع وسادة خلف ظهره لأني رأيته غير مستند فتنحى عن الوسادة قليلا فتوهمت أنه توقى الوسادة لأنه لم يكن عليه خرقة أو سجادة فقال لا أريد الاستناد فتأملت بعد حاله فكان لا يستند إلى شئ
Among them is Abu Ali al-Daqqaq al-Naysaburi, the shaykh of Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri, may Allah have mercy on him. The shaykh Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Ghafir ibn Ismail ibn Abd al-Ghafir al-Farisi wrote from Naysabur, saying: Al-Hasan ibn Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Ishaq ibn Abd al-Rahim ibn Ahmad Abu Ali al-Daqqaq was the tongue of his time and the imam of his era, of Naysaburi origin. He learned Arabic and acquired knowledge of usul, and went out to Marw and studied fiqh there, studied under al-Khidri, and repeated lessons under shaykh Abu Bakr al-Qaffal al-Marwazi in the lessons of al-Khidri, and excelled in it. When he had heard what he needed of the sciences, he took to action and followed the path of tasawwuf, and accompanied the master Abu al-Qasim al-Nasrabadhi. He died in Dhu al-Hijjah of the year four hundred and five. The shaykh Abu al-Muzaffar Abd al-Munim ibn Abd al-Karim ibn Hawazin al-Qushayri informed us in Naysabur, saying: My father, the master Abu al-Qasim, may Allah have mercy on him, said: At the beginning of my connection with the master Abu Ali, he arranged a gathering for me in the mosque of al-Mutarriz. I asked his permission at one time to go out to Nasa, and he permitted me. One day I was walking with him on the way to his gathering, and it occurred to me: 'If only he would deputize for me in the gatherings during my absence.' Then he turned to me and said: 'I will deputize for you during your absence in holding the gathering.' I walked a little, and it occurred to me that he was ill and it would be difficult for him to deputize for me two days a week; if only he would limit it to one day a week. Then he turned to me and said: 'If it is not possible for me two days a week, I will deputize once a week.' I walked a little, and a third thing occurred to me, and he turned to me and declared the information definitively. He said: The master Abu Ali, may Allah have mercy on him, would not lean on anything. One day he was in a gathering, and I wanted to place a cushion behind his back because I saw him not leaning, so he moved away from the cushion a little. I imagined that he avoided the cushion because there was no cloth or prayer rug on it, but he said: 'I do not want to lean.' Later I reflected on his state, and he would not lean on anything.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry. The author first gives the name and affiliation of Abu Ali al-Daqqaq as the shaykh of Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri. Then he quotes a letter from Abd al-Ghafir al-Farisi detailing his lineage: al-Hasan ibn Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Ishaq ibn Abd al-Rahim ibn Ahmad. He is described as 'the tongue of his time and the imam of his era', indicating his eloquence and scholarly authority. He was of Naysaburi origin, studied Arabic and usul (principles of jurisprudence), traveled to Marw to study fiqh, studied under al-Khidri, and assisted Abu Bakr al-Qaffal al-Marwazi in al-Khidri's lessons. After acquiring the necessary sciences, he turned to practice and followed the path of tasawwuf, accompanying the master Abu al-Qasim al-Nasrabadhi. His death is recorded in Dhu al-Hijjah 405 AH. The second part of the entry is an anecdote from Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri, narrated through his son. It illustrates Abu Ali's spiritual insight: when al-Qushayri wished that Abu Ali would deputize for him during his absence, Abu Ali immediately turned and said he would. When al-Qushayri later thought it would be better to limit it to one day, Abu Ali again responded. When a third thought occurred, Abu Ali again turned and declared the information definitively. This shows his ability to know the thoughts of others (kashf). The anecdote also notes that Abu Ali never leaned on anything, and when al-Qushayri tried to place a cushion behind him, he refused, saying he did not want to lean. This indicates his constant state of reliance on Allah and detachment from physical supports.

Key Terms
أبو علي الدقاق
أبو القاسم القشيري
النيسابوري
التصوف
أبو القاسم النصر أباذى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu Ali al-Daqqaq was purely a mystic without formal Islamic scholarship.
Correction: The biography explicitly states he studied Arabic, usul, and fiqh, and excelled in fiqh before turning to tasawwuf.

Checkpoint
In what year did Abu Ali al-Daqqaq die?
A) 405 AH
B) 415 AH
C) 395 AH
D) 425 AH

Key Takeaway
Abu Ali al-Daqqaq was a scholar who mastered exoteric sciences before devoting himself to the spiritual path, demonstrating that true tasawwuf is built upon sound knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The anecdote shows that Abu Ali al-Daqqaq knew the unspoken thoughts of his student. Do you truly believe that Allah knows every thought that passes through your mind, even those you never voice? If you did, would you still entertain the same secret thoughts, doubts, or sins? Commit today to whisper a specific thought you've hidden and repent from it now.
?
Abu Ali never leaned on any physical support, out of complete trust in Allah. What are you leaning on that is not Allah—your job, your health, your reputation, your family? Which one of these 'cushions' are you most afraid to lose? Before you sleep tonight, choose one and consciously rely on Allah for it, and note how it feels.
?
He combined deep knowledge of fiqh and usul with sincere spiritual practice. Is your pursuit of Islamic knowledge evenly matched by your action and spiritual state? What is one area where you have more knowledge than practice? Write down a specific, small action you will take this week to apply what you know.

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner
6 min
The Life and Education of al-Hakim al-Naysaburi
Ibn Asakir details al-Hakim's lineage, birth, and scholarly formation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early life, education, and scholarly network of al-Hakim al-Naysaburi, one of the most prominent hadith masters of the 4th century AH.

Core Idea
Al-Hakim's education spanned Khurasan, Iraq, and the Hijaz, with nearly 2,000 teachers.

Before we examine al-Hakim's impressive list of works, it is essential to appreciate the breadth of his scholarly training. This opening section of his biography traces his origins, his birth in 321 AH, and the vast network of teachers he studied under across the Islamic world. Understanding this foundation helps us see why his later contributions were so authoritative.

Guided Path
1
Read the biographical introduction
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ibn Asakir introduces al-Hakim with his full name, honorifics, and precise birth date.
The date is given as Monday, 3 Rabi' al-Awwal 321 AH.
2
Map the teachers by region
Identify the regions mentioned (Khurasan, al-Jibal, Iraq, Hijaz) and the teachers listed in each. Reflect on the geographical scope of his studies.
Al-Jibal refers to the mountainous region of western Iran.
3
Note the range of subjects
Pay attention to his studies beyond hadith: he also learned Qur'an recitation and fiqh under noted scholars. This shows his comprehensive training.
4
Reflect on the scale of his education
The text states his teacher dictionary includes nearly 2,000 shaykhs. Consider what this implies about the scholarly culture of the time.

Among them is al-Hakim Abu Abd Allah ibn al-Bayyi' al-Naysaburi al-Hafiz, may Allah have mercy on him. Muhammad ibn Abd Allah ibn Hamduwayh ibn Nu'aym ibn al-Hakam al-Hafiz, the imam of hadith scholars in his era. His birth was on the morning of Monday, the third of Rabi' al-Awwal in the year three hundred and twenty-one. He heard (hadith) in Khurasan from Abu al-Abbas ibn Ya'qub, Abu Abd Allah al-Saffar, Abu al-Abbas al-Mahbubi, and their class; in al-Jibal from Abu Hamdan al-Jallab and Abu Ja'far ibn Ubayd al-Hafiz al-Hamadhani; in Iraq from Abu Amr ibn al-Sammak and Ibn Uqba al-Shaybani and their class; in the Hijaz from Abu Yahya Abd Allah ibn Yazid al-Muqri' and Abu Ishaq ibn Faras al-Maliki and their peers. It is not possible to enumerate his shaykhs, for his mu'jam of his shaykhs includes close to two thousand men. He recited the Qur'an to al-Siram and Ibn al-Imam in Naysabur, and in Iraq to Ibn Ali ibn al-Naqqar al-Kufi and Abu Isa Bakkar al-Baghdadi. He studied fiqh under the imams: Abu Ali ibn Abi Hurayra in Iraq, Abu al-Walid Hissan ibn Muhammad al-Qurashi, and Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Sulayman al-Hanafi. I heard him say: 'I drank Zamzam water and asked Allah to grant me excellence in authorship.' So from his authored works that are heard (i.e., transmitted) and in the hands of the people, there reached one thousand five hundred parts, among them: al-Sahihan, al-'Ilal, al-Amali, Fawa'id al-Nuskh, Fawa'id al-Khurasaniyyin, Amali al-Ashiyyat, al-Talkhis, al-Abwab, and Tarajim al-Shuyukh. As for the books which he uniquely produced: Ma'rifat Anwa' Ulum al-Hadith, Tarikh Ulama Ahl Naysabur, Kitab Muzakki al-Akhbar, al-Madkhal ila Ilm al-Sahih, Kitab al-Iklil fi Dala'il al-Nubuwwa, al-Mustadrak ala al-Sahihayn, Ma Tafarrada bi-Ikhtiraj kull Wahid min al-Imamayn, Fada'il al-Shafi'i, Tarajim al-Musnad ala Shart al-Sahihayn, and others. He dictated (hadith) in Transoxiana in the year fifty-five and in Iraq in the year...

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry. The author identifies al-Hakim Abu Abd Allah ibn al-Bayyi' al-Naysaburi al-Hafiz (the memorizer) and gives his full name: Muhammad ibn Abd Allah ibn Hamduwayh ibn Nu'aym ibn al-Hakam. The biography lists his teachers by region: Khurasan (Abu al-Abbas ibn Ya'qub, Abu Abd Allah al-Saffar, Abu al-Abbas al-Mahbubi), al-Jibal (the mountains region: Abu Hamdan al-Jallab, Abu Ja'far ibn Ubayd al-Hamadhani), Iraq (Abu Amr ibn al-Sammak, Ibn Uqba al-Shaybani), and the Hijaz (Abu Yahya al-Muqri', Abu Ishaq ibn Faras al-Maliki). It notes that his mu'jam (dictionary of shaykhs) contains nearly two thousand teachers, showing his extensive network. He studied Quran recitation under al-Siram and Ibn al-Imam in Naysabur, and in Iraq under Ibn Ali al-Naqqar al-Kufi and Abu Isa Bakkar al-Baghdadi. He studied fiqh under Abu Ali ibn Abi Hurayra, Abu al-Walid al-Qurashi, and Abu Sahl al-Hanafi. A personal anecdote: al-Hakim said he drank Zamzam water and asked Allah to grant him excellence in authorship, which was answered. His authored works reached 1,500 parts (juz'), including: al-Sahihan (i.e., Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim? No, these are his own works titled 'al-Sahihan'? Actually al-Hakim wrote al-Mustadrak, and here he mentions al-Sahihan among his works—possibly meaning his own two Sahih collections? But it's more likely that 'al-Sahihan' refers to his works on the two Sahihs, perhaps his book on the conditions of Bukhari and Muslim. The text lists many works. He also uniquely authored books on hadith sciences, history, proofs of prophethood, etc. The biography ends mid-sentence noting he dictated in Transoxiana in 355 AH and in Iraq in a year that is cut off.

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner

The Prolific Works of al-Hakim al-Naysaburi
Ibn Asakir recounts al-Hakim's prayer for authorship and enumerates his numerous works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the extraordinary scholarly output of al-Hakim al-Naysaburi, including his most famous works like al-Mustadrak, and understand how his prayer for excellence in authorship was answered.

Core Idea
Al-Hakim authored 1,500 parts, with unique contributions in hadith methodology and history.

After surveying al-Hakim's education, we now turn to the fruit of his lifelong dedication: his massive body of written works. The passage describes how he prayed for excellence in authorship after drinking Zamzam water, and then lists a staggering number of works, many of which became foundational in hadith sciences. This lesson highlights his productivity and the scope of his legacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Zamzam story
Begin by reading the anecdote about drinking Zamzam water. Reflect on its significance: a personal prayer for scholarly productivity.
This story humanizes al-Hakim and connects his works to divine blessing.
2
Note the scale of his output
The text says his authored works reached 1,500 parts (ajza'). Compare this to modern book volumes to appreciate the magnitude.
A 'juz'' is typically a fascicle; 1,500 is extraordinary.
3
Identify the two categories of works
The passage distinguishes between works that were 'heard' (transmitted) and those he 'uniquely produced.' Look for the phrase 'فأما الكتب التي تفرد بإخراجها' to see the latter.
The first category includes widely transmitted works; the second are his original compositions.
4
Review the list of unique works
Pay attention to the titles: Ma'rifat Anwa' Ulum al-Hadith (a key work on hadith sciences), Tarikh Naysabur (history), al-Mustadrak (the most famous), etc.
Al-Mustadrak is his attempt to supplement Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim.

منهم الحاكم أبو عبد الله بن البيع النيسابوري الحافظ رحمه الله ) محمد نافلة قرأت بخط الشيخ أبي الحسن على بن سليمان التميمي ما ذكر أنه وقع إليه عن أبي حازم عمر بن أحمد بن إبراهيم الحافظ العبدوى قال : الإمام أبو عبد الله محمد بن عبد الله بن حمدويه بن نعيم بن الحكم الحافظ إمام أهل الحديث في عصره ، مولده صبيحة يوم الأثنين الثالث من شهر ربيع الأول سنة إحدى وعشرين وثلثمائة سمع بخراسان أبا العباس بن يعقوب وأبا عبد الله الصفار وأبا العباس المحبوبي وطبقتهم وبالجبال أبا بن حمدان الجلاب وأبا جعفر بن عبيد الحافظ الهمذاني ، وبالعراق أبا عمرو بن السماك وابن عقبة الشيباني وطبقتهما وبالحجاز أبا يحيى عبد الله بن يزيد المقرى وأبا إسحق بن فراس المالكي وأقرانهما وليس يمكن حصر شيوخه فإن معجمه على شيوخه يقرب من الفى رجل قرأ القرآن على الصرام وابن الإمام بنيسابور بالعراق على ابن على بن النقار الكوفي وأبى عیسى بكار البغدادي ، وتفقه عند الأئمة أبى على ابن أبي هريرة بالعراق وابى الوليد حسان بن محمد القرشي وأبي سهل محمد بن سليمان الحنفى سمعته يقول شربت ماء زمزم وسألت الله أن يرزقني حسن التصنيف فوقع من تصانيفه المسموعة في أيدى الناس ما يبلغ الـفـاً وخمسمائة جزء منها ( الصحيحان ) و ( العلل ) و ( الأمالي ) و ( فوائد النسخ ) و ( فوائد الخراسانيين ) و ( أمالي العشــيــات ) و ( التلخيص ) والأبواب و ) تراجم الشيوخ ) فأما الكتب التي تفرد بإخراجها فمعرفة أنواع علوم الحدیث و ( تاریخ علماء أهل نيسابور ) وكتاب ( مزكى الأخبار ) و ( المدخل إلى علم الصحيح ) وكتاب الأكليل في دلائل النبوة و ( المستدرك على الصحيحين ) و ( ما تفرد بإخراجه كل واحد من الأمامين ( و ) فضائل الشافعي ( و ) تراجم المسند على شرط الصحيحين ) . وغير ذلك أملى بما وراء النهر سنة خمس وخمسين وبالعراق سنة سبع وستين ولازمه ابن المظفر والدارقطني وأملى ببغداد والري مدة من من المشايخ أحمد بن أبي عثمان الحيرى الزاهد بن سمع حفظه محمد صلى عنه الزاهد والإمام أبو بكر القفال الشاشى وأبو أحمد بن مطرف والسيد أبو بن زباره العلوي وأبو عبد الله العصمى وأبو أحمد بن شعيب المزكي وأبو إسحق إبراهيم بن محمد بن يحيى ومن شيوخ العراق ابن أبى دارم وابن مظفر والدارقطني وابن القصار الرازى إمام أهل الزى قلد القضاء يعني بنسا سنة تسع وخمسين في أيام حشمة السامانية ووزارة العتبى ودخل الخليل بن أحمد السجزى القاضى على أبي جعفر العتبي يوم الثاني من مفارقته الحضرة فقال هنا الله الشيخ فقد جهز إلى نسا ثلاثمائة ألف حديث لرسول الله الله عليه وسلم فتهلل وجهه وقلد بعد ذلك قضاء جرجان فامتنع ، وكان الأمير أبو الحسن يستعين برأيه وينفذه للسفارة بينهم وبين البويهية . فأما مذاكرته فذاكر الجعابي وأبا جعفر الهمذاني وأبا على الحافظ وكان يقبل عليه من بين أقرانه قال وسمعت أبا أحمد الحافظ يقول إن كان رجل يقعد مكانى فهو أبو عبد الله صحب مشايخ التصوف أبا عمرو بن نجيد وأبا الحسن البوشنجى وأبا سعيد أحمد بن يعقوب الثقفي وأبا نصر الصفار وأبا القسم الرازي وبالعراق جعفر بن نصير وأقرانه ، وبالحجاز أبا عمرو الزجاجى وجعفر ابن إبراهيم الحذاء وكان يكثر الاختلاف إلى الشيخ أبي عثمان المغربي الله سمعت مشايخنا يقولون كان الشيخ أبو بكر بن إسحق وأبو الوليد يرجعان إلى أبي عبد الله في السؤال عن الجرح والتعديل وعلل الحديث وصحيحه وسقیمه قال وسمعت السلمى يقول كتبت على ظهر جزء من حديث أبي الحسين الحجاجي الحافظ فأخذ القلم وضرب على الحافظ وقال أيش أحفظ أنا ، أبو عبد ابن البياع أحفظ منى وأنا لم أر من الحفاظ إلا أبا على الحافظ وابن عقدة وسمعته يقول سألت الدارقطني أيهما أحفظ ابن مندة أو ابن البيع فقال ابن البيع أتقن حفظاً قال أبو حازم أقمت عند الشيخ أبي عبد الله العصمى قريبا من ثلاث سنين ولم أر في جملة مشايخنا أتقن منه ولا أكثر تنقيرا فكان إذا شكل عليه بشئ أمرني أن أكتب إلى الحاكم أبي عبد الله فإذا ورد جواب كتابه حكم به وقطع بقوله انتخب على المشايخ خمسين سنة .
Among them is al-Hakim Abu Abdullah ibn al-Bayyi' al-Naysaburi, the memorizer (al-hafiz), may Allah have mercy on him. I read in the handwriting of Sheikh Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Sulayman al-Tamimi what he mentioned that was conveyed to him from Abu Hazim Umar ibn Ahmad ibn Ibrahim al-Hafiz al-Abdawi: He said: The Imam Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Hamdawayh ibn Nu'aym ibn al-Hakam al-Hafiz, the Imam of the people of hadith in his time, was born on the morning of Monday, the third of Rabi' al-Awwal in the year 321 AH. He heard (hadith) in Khurasan from Abu al-Abbas ibn Ya'qub, Abu Abdullah al-Saffar, Abu al-Abbas al-Mahbubi, and their class; in the mountains from Abu ibn Hamdan al-Jallab, Abu Ja'far ibn Ubayd al-Hafiz al-Hamadhani; in Iraq from Abu Amr ibn al-Sammak, Ibn Uqba al-Shaybani, and their class; in the Hijaz from Abu Yahya Abdullah ibn Yazid al-Muqri', Abu Ishaq ibn Firas al-Maliki, and their peers. His teachers cannot be enumerated, for his dictionary (mu'jam) regarding his teachers includes nearly two thousand men. He recited the Quran to al-Siram and Ibn al-Imam in Naysabur, and in Iraq to Ibn Ali ibn al-Naqqar al-Kufi and Abu Isa Bakkar al-Baghdadi. He studied jurisprudence (tafaqqaha) under the Imams: Abu Ali ibn Abi Hurayra in Iraq, Abu al-Walid Hassan ibn Muhammad al-Qurashi, and Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Sulayman al-Hanafi. I heard him say: 'I drank Zamzam water and asked Allah to grant me excellence in authorship, so from his authored works that are heard among the people there amount to one thousand five hundred volumes, among them: al-Sahihan, al-'Ilal, al-Amali, Fawa'id al-Naskh, Fawa'id al-Khurasaniyyin, Amali al-'Ashiyyat, al-Talkhis, al-Abwab, Tarajim al-Shuyukh. As for the books that he uniquely produced: Ma'rifat Anwa' Ulum al-Hadith, Tarikh 'Ulama' Ahl Naysabur, Muzakki al-Akhbar, al-Madkhal ila 'Ilm al-Sahih, Kitab al-Iklil fi Dala'il al-Nubuwwa, al-Mustadrak 'ala al-Sahihayn, Ma tafarrada bi-ikhrajihi kullu wahid min al-Imamayn, Fada'il al-Shafi'i, Tarajim al-Musnad 'ala Shart al-Sahihayn. And other than that. He dictated (amla) beyond the river (Transoxiana) in the year 355 AH and in Iraq in the year 367 AH. Ibn al-Muzaffar and al-Daraqutni kept his company. He dictated in Baghdad and Rayy for a duration, from the teachers: Ahmad ibn Abi Uthman al-Hayri al-Zahid, ... al-Zahid, and Imam Abu Bakr al-Qaffal al-Shashi, Abu Ahmad ibn Mutarrif, al-Sayyid Abu ibn Zabbara al-Alawi, Abu Abdullah al-Asami, Abu Ahmad ibn Shu'ayb al-Muzakki, Abu Ishaq Ibrahim ibn Muhammad ibn Yahya. And among the teachers of Iraq: Ibn Abi Darim, Ibn Muzaffar, al-Daraqutni, and Ibn al-Qassar al-Razi, the Imam of the people of Rayy. He was appointed to the judiciary (qada') in Nasa in the year 359 AH during the days of the Samanid state and the vizierate of al-'Utbi. Al-Khalil ibn Ahmad al-Sijzi al-Qadi entered upon Abu Ja'far al-'Utbi on the second day of his departure from the presence, and said: 'Allah has honored the Sheikh, for three hundred thousand hadiths of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) have been dispatched to Nasa.' So his face beamed. He was later offered the judiciary of Jurjan but refused. The Amir Abu al-Hasan sought his advice and sent him as an envoy between them and the Buwayhids. As for his disputation (mudhakara), he engaged with al-Ju'abi, Abu Ja'far al-Hamadhani, and Abu Ali al-Hafiz, and he would favor him among his peers. He said: I heard Abu Ahmad al-Hafiz say: 'If any man is to sit in my place, it is Abu Abdullah.' He accompanied the Sufi masters: Abu Amr ibn Najid, Abu al-Hasan al-Bushanji, Abu Sa'id Ahmad ibn Ya'qub al-Thaqafi, Abu Nasr al-Saffar, Abu al-Qasim al-Razi; in Iraq: Ja'far ibn Nasir and his peers; in the Hijaz: Abu Amr al-Zajjaji and Ja'far ibn Ibrahim al-Hadhdha'. He frequently visited Sheikh Abu Uthman al-Maghribi. I heard our teachers say: Sheikh Abu Bakr ibn Ishaq and Abu al-Walid would refer to Abu Abdullah in questions about al-Jarh wa al-Ta'dil, the defects of hadith, and its authentic and weak. He said: I heard al-Sulami say: 'I wrote on the back of a volume of hadith of Abu al-Husayn al-Hajjaji al-Hafiz, so he took the pen and struck out al-Hafiz and said: 'What do I know? Abu Abdullah ibn al-Bayyi' knows more than I; I have not seen among the memorizers except Abu Ali al-Hafiz and Ibn Uqda.' I heard him say: 'I asked al-Daraqutni: Who is more retentive (ahfaz), Ibn Mandah or Ibn al-Bayyi'? He said: Ibn al-Bayyi' is more precise in retention (atqan hifzan).' Abu Hazim said: 'I stayed with Sheikh Abu Abdullah al-Asami for nearly three years, and I have not seen among all our teachers anyone more precise or more critical (antaqu). Whenever he was confused about something, he would order me to write to al-Hakim Abu Abdullah, and when the reply to his letter arrived, he would rule by it and accept his statement absolutely.' He (al-Hakim) selected (intakhaba) from the teachers for fifty years.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive biographical entry on the eminent hadith scholar and historian al-Hakim Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Abdullah al-Naysaburi, commonly known as Ibn al-Bayyi'. The biography begins with his full name and his birth on Monday, 3 Rabi' al-Awwal 321 AH. It then enumerates his teachers across various regions: Khurasan, the mountains (al-Jibal), Iraq, and the Hijaz, noting that his teacher dictionary includes nearly two thousand men. His studies included Quranic recitation and jurisprudence under several prominent scholars. The narrative then highlights his famous supplication at Zamzam, which he attributes to his prolific authorship; he authored some 1,500 volumes, including major works like al-Sahihan (a collection of authentic hadith), al-'Ilal, al-Amali, and numerous others. Specifically, the text lists books that he uniquely produced, such as Ma'rifat Anwa' Ulum al-Hadith, Tarikh 'Ulama' Ahl Naysabur, al-Mustadrak 'ala al-Sahihayn, and others. His activities as a teacher and dictator of hadith are recorded, with years and locations: he dictated in Transoxiana in 355 AH and in Iraq in 367 AH, and had a long period in Baghdad and Rayy. The biography lists a number of his students and associates, including notable figures like al-Daraqutni and Ibn al-Muzaffar. It also describes his appointment as judge in Nasa in 359 AH, which pleased him greatly upon hearing that 300,000 hadiths had been sent to that city, and his later refusal of the judgeship of Jurjan. The text notes his role as an advisor and envoy for the Amir Abu al-Hasan to the Buwayhids. His scholarly prominence is illustrated by the fact that masters like Abu Bakr ibn Ishaq and Abu al-Walid referred to him for questions on hadith criticism (al-Jarh wa al-Ta'dil) and the defects of hadith. Al-Sulami's anecdote shows al-Hakim's humility: when al-Sulami wrote on a volume attributing the title 'al-Hafiz' to another scholar, that scholar struck it out and deferred to Ibn al-Bayyi' as more knowledgeable. Similarly, al-Daraqutni judged Ibn al-Bayyi' as more precise in retention compared to Ibn Mandah. The passage concludes with Abu Hazim's testimony of al-Hakim's meticulousness and authority; when a difficult issue arose, he would write to al-Hakim and accept his written response as decisive. Finally, it notes that al-Hakim engaged in intikhab (selection of hadith) from the teachers for fifty years, underscoring his lifelong dedication to hadith scholarship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a hadith scholar only needs to learn from a few teachers in his home region.
Correction: Al-Hakim's education shows that the great hadith scholars traveled extensively and collected knowledge from hundreds or even thousands of teachers across the Islamic world.

Checkpoint
How many teachers did al-Hakim's teacher dictionary contain?
A) About 500
B) About 1,000
C) Close to 2,000
D) More than 3,000

Key Takeaway
Al-Hakim's studies under nearly 2,000 teachers across multiple regions demonstrate the immense travel and effort required to master hadith in his era.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Hakim traveled across four major regions to learn from nearly 2,000 teachers. Where is the 'region' of Islamic knowledge that you have been avoiding or neglecting? What is one step you can take this week to enter that region?
?
The source mentions that al-Hakim's teachers include specific individuals like Abu al-Abbas ibn Ya'qub and Abu Ja'far ibn Ubayd. Do you even know the names of your own teachers? Write down the names of three teachers who have taught you something beneficial about your faith. What have you done to thank them?
?
If you were given the chance to sit at the feet of al-Hakim, would you be willing to travel the same distances he did? What sacrifices have you made—or are you willing to make—to gain deep understanding of your religion? Be honest: what is your excuse?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner
5 min
The Prolific Writings of al-Hakim al-Naysaburi
Ibn Asakir describes al-Hakim's authorship and lists his major works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the vast output of al-Hakim's scholarly works, which included approximately 1,500 volumes and many landmark books.

Core Idea
Al-Hakim authored 1,500 volumes, including the famous al-Mustadrak 'ala al-Sahihayn.

After establishing his extensive education, Ibn Asakir turns to al-Hakim's own scholarly production. This passage recounts a personal story about drinking Zamzam water and praying for excellence in authorship, followed by an impressive list of his works. The narrative shows that his prolific writing was directly tied to his devotion and effort.

Guided Path
1
Read the Zamzam story
Read about al-Hakim drinking Zamzam and his prayer. Consider the significance of this act in Islamic tradition.
2
Identify the total number of volumes
Find the number of volumes he authored. Notice the text says 'one thousand and five hundred parts' (juz').
3
List the unique books
The passage enumerates books that al-Hakim 'uniquely produced.' Write down the titles mentioned, including al-Mustadrak 'ala al-Sahihayn.

الصحيحين ) و ( ما تفرد بإخراجه كل واحد من الأمامين ( و ) فضائل الشافعي ( و ) تراجم المسند على شرط الصحيحين ) . وغير ذلك أملى بما وراء النهر سنة خمس وخمسين وبالعراق سنة سبع وستين ولازمه ابن المظفر والدارقطني وأملى ببغداد والري مدة من من المشايخ أحمد بن أبي عثمان الحيرى الزاهد بن سمع حفظه محمد صلى عنه الزاهد والإمام أبو بكر القفال الشاشى وأبو أحمد بن مطرف والسيد أبو بن زباره العلوي وأبو عبد الله السامانية ووزارة العتبى ودخل الخليل بن أحمد السجزى القاضى على أبي جعفر العتبي يوم الثاني من مفارقته الحضرة فقال هنا الله الشيخ فقد جهز إلى نسا ثلاثمائة ألف حديث لرسول الله الله عليه وسلم فتهلل وجهه وقلد بعد ذلك قضاء جرجان فامتنع ، وكان الأمير أبو الحسن يستعين برأيه وينفذه للسفارة بينهم وبين البويهية . فأما مذاكرته فذاكر الجعابي وأبا جعفر الهمذاني وأبا على الحافظ وكان يقبل عليه من بين أقرانه قال وسمعت أبا أحمد الحافظ يقول إن كان رجل يقعد مكانى فهو أبو عبد الله صحب مشايخ التصوف أبا عمرو بن نجيد وأبا الحسن البوشنجى وأبا سعيد أحمد بن يعقوب الثقفي وأبا نصر الصفار وأبا القسم الرازي وبالعراق جعفر بن نصير وأقرانه ، وبالحجاز أبا عمرو الزجاجى وجعفر ابن إبراهيم الحذاء وكان يكثر الاختلاف إلى الشيخ أبي عثمان المغربي الله سمعت مشايخنا يقولون كان الشيخ أبو بكر بن إسحق وأبو الوليد يرجعان إلى أبي عبد الله في السؤال عن الجرح والتعديل وعلل الحديث وصحيحه وسقیمه قال وسمعت السلمى يقول كتبت على ظهر جزء من حديث أبي الحسين الحجاجي الحافظ فأخذ القلم وضرب على الحافظ وقال أيش أحفظ أنا ، أبو عبد ابن البياع أحفظ منى وأنا لم أر من الحفاظ إلا أبا على الحافظ وابن عقدة وسمعته يقول سألت الدارقطني أيهما أحفظ ابن مندة أو ابن البيع فقال ابن البيع أتقن حفظاً قال أبو حازم أقمت عند الشيخ أبي عبد الله العصمى قريبا من ثلاث سنين ولم أر في جملة مشايخنا أتقن منه ولا أكثر تنقيرا فكان إذا شكل عليه بشئ أمرني أن أكتب إلى الحاكم أبي عبد الله فإذا ورد جواب كتابه حكم به وقطع بقوله انتخب على المشايخ خمسين سنة .
Among them is al-Hakim Abu Abdullah ibn al-Bayyi' al-Naysaburi, the memorizer (al-hafiz), may Allah have mercy on him. I read in the handwriting of Sheikh Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Sulayman al-Tamimi what he mentioned that was conveyed to him from Abu Hazim Umar ibn Ahmad ibn Ibrahim al-Hafiz al-Abdawi: He said: The Imam Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Hamdawayh ibn Nu'aym ibn al-Hakam al-Hafiz, the Imam of the people of hadith in his time, was born on the morning of Monday, the third of Rabi' al-Awwal in the year 321 AH. He heard (hadith) in Khurasan from Abu al-Abbas ibn Ya'qub, Abu Abdullah al-Saffar, Abu al-Abbas al-Mahbubi, and their class; in the mountains from Abu ibn Hamdan al-Jallab, Abu Ja'far ibn Ubayd al-Hafiz al-Hamadhani; in Iraq from Abu Amr ibn al-Sammak, Ibn Uqba al-Shaybani, and their class; in the Hijaz from Abu Yahya Abdullah ibn Yazid al-Muqri', Abu Ishaq ibn Firas al-Maliki, and their peers. His teachers cannot be enumerated, for his dictionary (mu'jam) regarding his teachers includes nearly two thousand men. He recited the Quran to al-Siram and Ibn al-Imam in Naysabur, and in Iraq to Ibn Ali ibn al-Naqqar al-Kufi and Abu Isa Bakkar al-Baghdadi. He studied jurisprudence (tafaqqaha) under the Imams: Abu Ali ibn Abi Hurayra in Iraq, Abu al-Walid Hassan ibn Muhammad al-Qurashi, and Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Sulayman al-Hanafi. I heard him say: 'I drank Zamzam water and asked Allah to grant me excellence in authorship, so from his authored works that are heard among the people there amount to one thousand five hundred volumes, among them: al-Sahihan, al-'Ilal, al-Amali, Fawa'id al-Naskh, Fawa'id al-Khurasaniyyin, Amali al-'Ashiyyat, al-Talkhis, al-Abwab, Tarajim al-Shuyukh. As for the books that he uniquely produced: Ma'rifat Anwa' Ulum al-Hadith, Tarikh 'Ulama' Ahl Naysabur, Muzakki al-Akhbar, al-Madkhal ila 'Ilm al-Sahih, Kitab al-Iklil fi Dala'il al-Nubuwwa, al-Mustadrak 'ala al-Sahihayn, Ma tafarrada bi-ikhrajihi kullu wahid min al-Imamayn, Fada'il al-Shafi'i, Tarajim al-Musnad 'ala Shart al-Sahihayn. And other than that. He dictated (amla) beyond the river (Transoxiana) in the year 355 AH and in Iraq in the year 367 AH. Ibn al-Muzaffar and al-Daraqutni kept his company. He dictated in Baghdad and Rayy for a duration, from the teachers: Ahmad ibn Abi Uthman al-Hayri al-Zahid, ... al-Zahid, and Imam Abu Bakr al-Qaffal al-Shashi, Abu Ahmad ibn Mutarrif, al-Sayyid Abu ibn Zabbara al-Alawi, Abu Abdullah al-Asami, Abu Ahmad ibn Shu'ayb al-Muzakki, Abu Ishaq Ibrahim ibn Muhammad ibn Yahya. And among the teachers of Iraq: Ibn Abi Darim, Ibn Muzaffar, al-Daraqutni, and Ibn al-Qassar al-Razi, the Imam of the people of Rayy. He was appointed to the judiciary (qada') in Nasa in the year 359 AH during the days of the Samanid state and the vizierate of al-'Utbi. Al-Khalil ibn Ahmad al-Sijzi al-Qadi entered upon Abu Ja'far al-'Utbi on the second day of his departure from the presence, and said: 'Allah has honored the Sheikh, for three hundred thousand hadiths of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) have been dispatched to Nasa.' So his face beamed. He was later offered the judiciary of Jurjan but refused. The Amir Abu al-Hasan sought his advice and sent him as an envoy between them and the Buwayhids. As for his disputation (mudhakara), he engaged with al-Ju'abi, Abu Ja'far al-Hamadhani, and Abu Ali al-Hafiz, and he would favor him among his peers. He said: I heard Abu Ahmad al-Hafiz say: 'If any man is to sit in my place, it is Abu Abdullah.' He accompanied the Sufi masters: Abu Amr ibn Najid, Abu al-Hasan al-Bushanji, Abu Sa'id Ahmad ibn Ya'qub al-Thaqafi, Abu Nasr al-Saffar, Abu al-Qasim al-Razi; in Iraq: Ja'far ibn Nasir and his peers; in the Hijaz: Abu Amr al-Zajjaji and Ja'far ibn Ibrahim al-Hadhdha'. He frequently visited Sheikh Abu Uthman al-Maghribi. I heard our teachers say: Sheikh Abu Bakr ibn Ishaq and Abu al-Walid would refer to Abu Abdullah in questions about al-Jarh wa al-Ta'dil, the defects of hadith, and its authentic and weak. He said: I heard al-Sulami say: 'I wrote on the back of a volume of hadith of Abu al-Husayn al-Hajjaji al-Hafiz, so he took the pen and struck out al-Hafiz and said: 'What do I know? Abu Abdullah ibn al-Bayyi' knows more than I; I have not seen among the memorizers except Abu Ali al-Hafiz and Ibn Uqda.' I heard him say: 'I asked al-Daraqutni: Who is more retentive (ahfaz), Ibn Mandah or Ibn al-Bayyi'? He said: Ibn al-Bayyi' is more precise in retention (atqan hifzan).' Abu Hazim said: 'I stayed with Sheikh Abu Abdullah al-Asami for nearly three years, and I have not seen among all our teachers anyone more precise or more critical (antaqu). Whenever he was confused about something, he would order me to write to al-Hakim Abu Abdullah, and when the reply to his letter arrived, he would rule by it and accept his statement absolutely.' He (al-Hakim) selected (intakhaba) from the teachers for fifty years.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive biographical entry on the eminent hadith scholar and historian al-Hakim Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Abdullah al-Naysaburi, commonly known as Ibn al-Bayyi'. The biography begins with his full name and his birth on Monday, 3 Rabi' al-Awwal 321 AH. It then enumerates his teachers across various regions: Khurasan, the mountains (al-Jibal), Iraq, and the Hijaz, noting that his teacher dictionary includes nearly two thousand men. His studies included Quranic recitation and jurisprudence under several prominent scholars. The narrative then highlights his famous supplication at Zamzam, which he attributes to his prolific authorship; he authored some 1,500 volumes, including major works like al-Sahihan (a collection of authentic hadith), al-'Ilal, al-Amali, and numerous others. Specifically, the text lists books that he uniquely produced, such as Ma'rifat Anwa' Ulum al-Hadith, Tarikh 'Ulama' Ahl Naysabur, al-Mustadrak 'ala al-Sahihayn, and others. His activities as a teacher and dictator of hadith are recorded, with years and locations: he dictated in Transoxiana in 355 AH and in Iraq in 367 AH, and had a long period in Baghdad and Rayy. The biography lists a number of his students and associates, including notable figures like al-Daraqutni and Ibn al-Muzaffar. It also describes his appointment as judge in Nasa in 359 AH, which pleased him greatly upon hearing that 300,000 hadiths had been sent to that city, and his later refusal of the judgeship of Jurjan. The text notes his role as an advisor and envoy for the Amir Abu al-Hasan to the Buwayhids. His scholarly prominence is illustrated by the fact that masters like Abu Bakr ibn Ishaq and Abu al-Walid referred to him for questions on hadith criticism (al-Jarh wa al-Ta'dil) and the defects of hadith. Al-Sulami's anecdote shows al-Hakim's humility: when al-Sulami wrote on a volume attributing the title 'al-Hafiz' to another scholar, that scholar struck it out and deferred to Ibn al-Bayyi' as more knowledgeable. Similarly, al-Daraqutni judged Ibn al-Bayyi' as more precise in retention compared to Ibn Mandah. The passage concludes with Abu Hazim's testimony of al-Hakim's meticulousness and authority; when a difficult issue arose, he would write to al-Hakim and accept his written response as decisive. Finally, it notes that al-Hakim engaged in intikhab (selection of hadith) from the teachers for fifty years, underscoring his lifelong dedication to hadith scholarship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Hakim only wrote al-Mustadrak, but he authored many other important works.
Correction: He produced a vast corpus of 1,500 volumes covering hadith, history, and other sciences.

Checkpoint
How many volumes did al-Hakim's authored works amount to?
A) 500 volumes
B) 1,000 volumes
C) 1,500 volumes
D) 2,000 volumes

Key Takeaway
Al-Hakim's prayer at Zamzam for excellence in authorship was answered with an unparalleled output of 1,500 volumes, including the famous al-Mustadrak 'ala al-Sahihayn.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you sincerely asked Allah for excellence in a specific skill or area of your life, like al-Hakim did at Zamzam? What holds you back from making such a specific and ambitious dua?
?
Al-Hakim produced 1,500 volumes. What would it take for you to leave a legacy that serves the Ummah? Are you investing your time in something that will outlive you?
?
Do you rely more on your own intelligence and effort, or do you actively seek Allah's help and blessing in your work? How can you change that starting today?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner
5 min
Al-Hakim's Authority in Hadith Criticism
Ibn Asakir presents al-Hakim's teaching, judicial roles, and scholarly recognition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will recognize how al-Hakim's contemporaries, including the great hadith critic al-Daraqutni, regarded him as a foremost authority in hadith criticism.

Core Idea
Even al-Daraqutni considered al-Hakim more precise in retention than Ibn Mandah.

The final section of Ibn Asakir's biography reveals al-Hakim's standing in the scholarly community. Through multiple testimonies from peers and students, we see that he was not only a prolific author but also the go-to authority for jarh wa ta'dil (hadith criticism). The passage includes his teaching activities, judicial appointments, and the highest praise from contemporaries.

Guided Path
1
Read about his teaching and students
Notice the list of scholars who studied with him or accompanied him, including al-Daraqutni and Ibn al-Muzaffar.
2
Note his judicial and diplomatic roles
He was appointed judge in Nasa and later refused Jurjan. Also served as envoy. This shows his public stature.
3
Analyze the testimonies of his expertise
The passage includes statements from al-Sulami, al-Daraqutni, and Abu Hazim. Each testifies to his superior knowledge in hadith, especially in jarh wa ta'dil.
Pay attention to al-Daraqutni's comparison with Ibn Mandah.

الإمام أبو عبد الله محمد بن عبد الله بن حمدويه بن نعيم بن الحكم الحافظ إمام أهل الحديث في عصره ، مولده صبيحة يوم الأثنين الثالث من شهر ربيع الأول سنة إحدى وعشرين وثلثمائة سمع بخراسان أبا العباس بن تصانيفه المسموعة في أيدى الناس ما يبلغ الـفـاً وخمسمائة جزء منها ( الصحيحان ) و ( العلل ) و ( الأمالي ) و ( فوائد النسخ ) و ( فوائد الخراسانيين ) و ( أمالي العشــيــات ) و ( التلخيص ) والأبواب و )
